Í Ingenuity of Slovak Immigrants MORE SLOVAK AMERICAN INVENTORS By Anthony X. Sutherland, Ph.D. Many Slovak-Americans are familiar with or at least have heard about Father Joseph Murgas and his work in the development of the wireless telegraph And maybe a lew have seen the name of Stefan Banic m coiw^ijqh with the invention of the párachutŠ^here were a 11 few otheT-Sloíak American inven- * tors, who, although unknown today, were front page news some sixty o; seventy years ago. On January 4. 1915. Anton Uh- liarik from New York, formerly from Rajec in Trenom, received a United States patent lor an automobile radiator To this project Uhliarik devoted almost three years and a considerable amount of money. Jozef Kubovcik from Jednota Br. 37 in Kingston. Pa., received a government patent on June 5.1923 for a machine to hammer or put in place plaster boards. This work, ■ which took five men to accomplish, cou'd now be done with only two using this invention The cost of the machine was S20 00 but the value of the patent was estimated at $70,000 to S75.000 from which Kubovcik hoped to receive about $10.000 a year. Kubovcik. a coal ■ miner from Dúbrava. Spis, prayed day and night that the Holy Spirit would enlighten him so he could get out of the living grave of a coal mine A Jan Kolibas of McKeesport. Pa.. received a United States patent on February 2. 1916 for a sword that contained a shooting mechanism m the handle. The sword was light enough to be used in the sport of fendng but ar the same time had a sort of revolver in the handle Kolibas worked lor the National Tube Works in McKeesport during the day and at night experimented with things at home. He invented some sort of decorative iron box and also discovered a way to perfect the machines used at the factory where he worked. The company awarded him by giving him an easier position. Meanwhile, offers for his sword came in from all over the country Jan Kolibas was the brother of an organist in Jessup. Pa.. Juraj Kolibas. What became of these men and their inventions is unknown Perhaps someone can lurnish additional information At any rate, these inventions testify to the ingenuity and perseverance of the Slovak immigrant that is often overlooked CATHOLIC PRESS MONTH By Host Reverend Anthony G. Bosco, Bishop of Greensburg For T.S. Eliot. April was "the cruelest month." But many residents of 4 the northern hemisphere would probably give February its share ol votes as cruelest and bleakest month of the year even though Valentine’s Day with its hearts and flowers seeks to warm the winter chill But for Catholic journalists and their readers. February is important because it is Catholic press Month and directs our attention to that great blessing which is the Catholic press Catholic journalists, writers, and publishers, modest and unassuming . as they are, do not mind being given the shortest month of the year for their month It provides them with an annual opportunity to reflect upon their sacred task It reminds readers of the Catholic press to thank God lor this gift which keeps on giving. The theme lor Catholic Press Month 1988 is "Collaborators of Truth in the Service ol Justice. Fairness, and Love. The great gift of communica- r lion which God has given the human race sets us far above the animal kingdom and just a little below the angels. Since the gift comes from God and since our God is a God of truth, it follows that the gift must be used to communicate truth To use it lor any other purpose is to distort and debase this great gift. It s a rumor • that refuses to die Despite periodic denials by Vatican and Soviet officials that a trip is in the works, the query often pops from the lips of journalists with access to Pope John Paul II Part of the speculation can be at tnbuled Io wishful thinking by newsmen conjunng a tantalizing story: the Slavic leader ol the world's largest religion barnstorming through the Slavic superpower , which is officially atheist and ideologically committed to the elimination of belief in God Besides, the pope comes from Poland, a country under (he political domination of the Soviet Union. He would be a strong delender ol human rights and religious liberty visiting a country with a Woody record on both issues. Yet behind the rumors are some hard facts. Under Communist Par ty leader Mikhail Gorbachev, the Soviet Union is trying to change its international image as a closed, rigid society. Extremely visible church personalities such as Nobel Peace laureate Mother Teresa of Calcutta and Filipino Cardinal Jaime Sin of Manila have made highly publicized visils there. Parallel to these have been little noticed, but perhaps more significant. trips by other church figures such as Ukraimannte Bishop Andres Sapeiak of Argentina. FEBRUARY 3. 1988 NUMBER 5135 At Syracuse University's Genet Gallery “TREASURES OF SLOVAKIA”: A FEAST FOR THE EYES By Helene Cincebox An exotic garden has bloomed at Syracuse University’s Genet Gallery since November with a breathtaking display of some 300 pieces of Slovak folk dress and folk an ' Treasures of Slovakia is a feast for the eyes with color and pattern abounding — a testament to the incredible skill and artistry of Slovak village people Although not trained as arusts. the villagers designs, color sense and vanety of decoration are remarkable — inspiring today’s students, artists, lace makers, embroiderers, weavers and leather workers The exhibit has generated an unprececented amount of attention at the University with comments ranging from tsa pasta pro oci' (feast tor the eyes) to ' just fantastic!" It features folk dress from all corners of Slovakia Almost all of the 16 major counties are represented as well as the neighbor mg regions of Moravian-Slovakia and toe White Carpathians Plans are tor the exhibit to Havel to Wilkes-Barre and possibly to other museums so that many more people can learn about and take prde m the richness of Slovak hentage. Slovakia with its range upon range of mountains may be the richest area in the world in the variety of folk dress The mountain ranges kepi the villages isolated and folk dress developed quite differently even m neighboring villages. Many villages preserved ancient designs and motifs that were incorporated folk wear and wonderful customs and traditions were intertwined with the clothing Your dress told so much about you — whether you were rich or poor young or old. married or single Special clothing was created for the bnde and groom, for the new baby and new mother, for toe widow or soldier recruit and special customs came wtto This adornment Guest curators for "Treasures of Slovakia ' Helen Zemek Baine and her daughter Helene, along with Genet Gal'ery Director Tern Jones, have created an exhibit which shares the thrill of opening village dowry chests or peeking in a peasant’s cupboard to see the neatly stacked clothing that spans a life — irom toe christening doth to the clothes set aside for the burial, always the best' Pope John Paul Il's Message WORLD COMMUNICATIONS DAY Brothers and sisters, my dear (r ends the communicators 1 . CYRIL AND METHODIUS By Father Augustine Zan, M.Th. SS. Cyril and Methodius, Evangellzera and Educators among the Slava, and Co-Potrons of Europe Our Slovak ancestors were among the nations in Central Europe which, in the 8th century A 0 had not yel received the blessed boon ol conversion to Christianity Kmg Rastislav, ruler of Slovakia appealed to the court of Constantinople for missionaries who had a command ol the language of his Slovak people Emperor Michael tn assigned this mission to SS Cyril and Methodius, whose feast we celebrate February 14th. SS Cynt and Methodius were not only related by religious ties, they were brothers of the same family They arrived as missionaries to Slovakia in 863 In their effort to bring to oor-^ncestors a rebirth m Christ as speedily and as thoroughly afe possible they translated the Gospels and toe Liturgy into Slovak As often happens when someone successfully accomplishes a daring new venture, they were not wanting toe malcontents who brought charges of heresy against them m Rome The Pope summoned them to appear before him and when he had examined them and found the charges false he bleásed their mission and consecrated them bishops St Cyril, however leu ill m Rome and died and was buried there His brother St Methodius, returned to Slovakia as toe Papal Legate Despite remonstrances from Rome, his enemies kepi him three years in prison, treating him with studied insults and cruelty The conversion of the nation went on apace, however The people discerned toe Holy Spiril m their apostles SS Cynl and Methodius and recognized them as messengers of God who visibly blessed and supported them The entire nation accepted Christianity. Branch 553 And the center of this fraternalism is the Society s Jednota Club Room. 485 Morgan Ave. in Akron ail Yanatchko invites everyone to come to the club and taste the good Slovak food that is offered several days a week and to enjoy the Jednota fraternal spirit there. There is always singing, laughing, talking, and just old fashioned socializing The manager ol the dub room tor the past several years has been John Falatak. Boofe.Rébiew The Homing Spirit: A Pilgrimage of the Mind, of the Heart, of the Soul By Boly Cross Father John S. Dunne Reviewed by Joseph R. Thomas, NC News Service Any pilgrimage of the spirt - unlike a simple physical pilgrimage to some holy place such as Lourdes — is by its nature intensely personal And so it is with the pilgrimage recounted by Holy Cross Father John S. Dunne, a profes sor of theology at the University of Notre Dame. Bemg persona) it is not a pilgrimage that another could replicate, even if he or she had a mind to. For one thing, the pilgrimages (for rn fact there were three, although the title speaks ol a single pilgrimage) were, apparently, inciden lai to the three trips 1o the Holy Land undertaken by Father Dunne Io explore win other Christians. Moslems and Jews various aspects of belief and the spiritual hie. Which is not Io say that the spinlual insights recorded in this book were accidental, but simply to note that they were themes he explored on his own, apart from the dialogue, although it was the dialogue that made the exploration of those (homes in the Holy Land possible. Do you want to give the best ol yourself, your life, your taienls and above all your deep love ol God and your neighbor through PRAYER and WORK? This may be a fine distinction, but Father Dunne's bock is composed of so many fine distinctions that the reader who has had no experience with contemplation isn't likely to enjoy it. In one sense, it's like a travel book If you have a burning desire to visit Ireland rather than Alaska, you're not likely to choose a meditation on Alaska's white wilderness for personal enjoyment But if the mystical appeals to you. you'll find Father Dunne's book a challenge and a joy It's filled with images, nuances and ruminations on the meaning not only of words but of the Word and how it is possible :o be with God and yet to experience a-ioneness. The Homing Spin: is not an easy read, filled as it is with references to Kant. Newman. Buber. Hilell, Kierkegaard and ether thinkers. In some ways, too. it disappoints. Were the dialogues m which he participated so abstract that they deserve no more than passing mention as the occasion for his tops’5 Additionally, the structure of the book seems somewhat contrived It is easy enough to accept the emphasis on the mind in his first visit, a lime when he was searching for peace ol mind as a result of a toss. But did meditation on the other themes actually wail upon his return to (he Holy Land after a two-year and then a nine year lapse7 There is disappainlment too in the failure to confide any information whatsoever about the "loss'' lhal gave rise to his first search. This loss rs so central to the start of Father Dunne's pilgrimage that the failure to confide in the reader is puzzling in light of the intimacy ol succeeding chapters In fact, it gives rise to questions ot credibility. Still, there are a multitude ol insights here as Father Dunne sorts out his thinking and resolves the paradoxes that deep contemplation ol the divine is fikeiy to uncover. His questioning is a stimulation of our own questioning. The involved reader cannot but help to reflect deeply on the divinity, on longing. on oneness, on self, in mystery, on faih. on restlessness, and on the beyond. Here is a book (hat invites (he mind to race off on its own, Crossroad (New York. 1987) 132 pp. $12 95 Thomas Is editor in chief of The Christophers. University of Pittsburgh Presents Sigrid Piroch By Christine Metli Tlie Department of Slavic Languages at the University □1 Pittsburgh, together with the Pittsburgh Center for the Arts, will sponsor a lecture by the noted Slovak weaver. Sigrid Piroch, at 2 00 p.m., Sunday. March 20. 1988 in Dining Room A of the William Pitt Union on the Pitt campus The topic of the lecture will be "Slovak Textiles — Hand- spuns and Handwovens ol Slovakia." The fascinating and color- ful patterning of the various regrons of Slovakia will be presented m a slide presentation, followed by a demonstration of weaving techniques on a floor loom. Ms. Piroch is a proponent of the preservation of the Sovak handweaving tradition, and has done extensive research via museum and private collections on both technical and cultural information refated to Slovak textiles The lecture is free and open to the public, and will be followed by a reception. FEBRUARY 3. 1988 BENEDICTINE MONKS OF SAINT ANDREW ABBEY Christ remains active in His Church through the gift of the Holy Spirit inspiring us from within to love God with our whole heart and soul. By a special gilt of grace, men since the^arly days of the church have experienced a divine call to go beyond the renewal of life achieved in baptism and to seek more freedom frdflrOwwgbstacles to holiness in this world. JBen^ffctine monasticism is one form of this religious calling. Saint Benedict was a young Roman of the 5th century A.D. who sought perfection and greater union with God through a religious community separated from civil society. He organized the principles of the gospels into a "Rule for Monasteries'‘ which established a family style of life for monks under the guidance of a spiritual father, the Abbot. Prayer and work are the poles around which tlie Rule arranges the daily life of the monks The moderation and adaptability of the Holy Rule has insured the survival of Bencdic- tinism for 1500 years. Yet. the Benedictine vocation rests firmly upon the vows each monk professes. These solemn promises are: obedience |to the Abbot, the superior of the monastery), conversion of life (a reordering of priorities in a life of celibacy and poverty), and stability (a unique Benedictine vow that binds the monk to one particular monastery for life). St. Andrew Abbey is one of 45 Benedictine monasteries scattered across the United States. The abbey has 63 monks of which 40 are also ordained priests. Like most Benedictine communities tn this country, the Cleveland Benedictines are not a cloistered contemplative community, but balance the hidden life of prayer within the cloister with active apostolic works for the good of the Church. The Benedictine Monks came to Cleveland In 1922 to serve the spiritual needs of Slovak Catholic immigrants. The monastery is located on 16 acres of wooded property in a residential area on the southeast side of Cleveland about 20 minutes from downtown. Since 1927. the major apostolic work of the community has been to conduct Benedictine High School, a commuter, secondary school for 550 boys. The high school complex is located on the monastery grounds. Monks are involved in the school as teachers, administrators, coaches, cafeteria manager, nurse, bus driver, maintenance and other areas of service. Monks also hold jobs within the monastery itself. These include cook, tailor, auto mechanic, infirmarian, boilerman. printer, carpenter and other jobs necessary for the daily operation of the monastery. Priest-monks may be assigned to spend some time serving at one of the monastery 's four parishes. On weekends, priest-monks from the abbey assist sacramentally at local diocesan parishes- Wichin the monastery the monks meet dally in choir four times for the celebration of the Liturgy of the Hours. The morning com-^ munity Eucharist is the core of the monk's * * spiritual life. Certain meals and periods of the day have required silence to help the monks recollect themselves after a day's labors. Spiritual reading and meditation are also essential duties of the monastic vocation. Any baptized Catholic man with good-J health, average intelligence, and sincere religious faith may be called to the Benedictine life. Inquiries may be made at any tíme, though admission to training can only come after the candidate has had some experience In school or work after graduation from high school. The candidate may visit the community several nines before asking to be admitted as a postulant. The period of postulancy lasts about 3 months and gives both the candidate and the community a chance to know each other. The community will then vote to admit the postulant to the novitiate, a one year period of intense training that precedes first vows. For information write: Father Bede. OSB. Vocation Director. Saint Andrew Abbe}-. 2900 M.L. King Drive. Cleveland. Ohio 44104. TRUTH AT MY TABLE BY JOSEPH C. KRAJSA, KSGG A MONASTERY IN THE CITY Washington Letter The Pro-Life Congressional Agenda for 1988 WASHINGTON (NC) - From constitutional amendments to controversial civil rights legislation and the District of Columbia budget, the pro-life agenda piled up in Congress in 1987. As the annual March for Life took place Jan. 22. much of that agenda awaited further action. According to Douglas Johnson. legislative director of the National Right to Life Commit- tee. "as usual, several strong anti abortion bills and constitutional amendments were introduced" in 1987. when the 100th Congress began its two- year term. Johnson, writing in the Jan. 22 issue of the National Right to Life News, said that "although there was a great deal of activity on pro-life issues during the 1987 congressional session,- most of it occurred at the committee level" and only two issues got to the House or Senate floor. One was the Supreme Court nomination of Judge Robert Bork, which, to pro-lifers' dismay. the Senate defeated. The other was the was the question of whether the District of Columbia budget, which is devised by the city government By Liz Schevtchuk and involves local tax money but is ratified by Congress, should include abortion funding. The House in 1987 said "no." the Senate said "yes " The funding ultimately was approved. but further debate may ensue. Johnson described the Supreme Court vacancy as "the most important pro-life contest" of 1987 and the Senate's scuttling of Bork's nomination as "a major political defeat for the pro-life movement." Following that rejection, the nomination process continued, with a Senate vote on the latest nominee. Judge Anthony Kennedy. still pending. At least 14 proposed amendments to the Constitution dealing with abortion were introduced during 1987. Yet, by the end of Ocotber. there had been "no action at the committee level" in either House or Senate on any of them and there is “no action anticipated" in 1988 either, according to the National Committee for a Human Life Amendment. Johnson noted that for years the leadership of the House has "bottled up in hostile committees all strong pro-life bills and constitutional amendments'" and that "pro-abortion forces now enjoying an advantage in the Senate" thwart anti-abortion chances there. One of the more controvcr sial bills — even pro-lifers debated it — is the "superbill” or "president's bill." Introduced at the behest of the White House. The bill would declare that "abortion takes the life of an unborn child" and that "a right to abortion is not secured by the Constitution of the United States.” It also states chat "the Supreme Court erred" in its 1973 abortion decision. The bill also would permanently ban federal funding of abortion, except in cases where the woman's life was endangered by pregnancy, and would prohibit family planning agencies that provide abortion procedures or abortion referrals from receiving federal Title X family planning grants. But. as in the case of the proposed consltutlonal amendments. there is "no action anticipated" in 1988 on the "superbill." according to the National Committee for a Human Life Amendment. The Title X family planning program likewise could prompt further debate. One fight has been over new Reagan administration regulations to prevent Title X recipient agencies from providing abortion counseling or referrals. The agencies already are forbidden to use Title X money In "programs where abortion is a method of family planning" but pro-lifers have long complained about close ties between family planning clinics and abortion clinics. Efforts in Congress to stop Reagan failed in December. But another Title X debate looms over a bill to re-authorize Title X for four years. Illusions and Realities CAN REAGAN SAVE DEMOCRATS? By Michael Novak Ironically, concerning Nicaragua, only President Reagan can save the Democratic Party. Tending toward suicide as it recently has. my beloved Democratic Partv — the party of FDR in World War II; Harry Truman in Italy. Greece, and Korea; and the JFK of "bear any burden, pay any price" — is no longer able to save itself. Like Gary Hart with Donna Rice, the party is risking its reputation in a dalliance with Leninist Nicaragua. Democrats used to be anti-communist and pro-democratic. The years of World War 1 were probably the most challenging in Slovak American history. Slovak Americans followed very closely events taking place in Europe, especially those in Eastern Europe. As in any war. there was a certain amount of uncertainty, an uncertainty about what was really going on and an uncertainty about the outcome. This natural uncertainty affected the First Catholic Slovak Union. Because Jednota was a benefit society', the supreme officers were concerned about the possibility of a large number of deaths due to the war and the organization's ability to pay all the insurance. Michal Senko, then Supreme Secretary, said that the organ! zation had sufficent funds only for peacetime. He added that if 5.000 or 6.000 members were called to service and if there were one hundred deaths, the Society would have to pay S10O.Ó0O in benefits. The Societ}" could afford to pay a large number of death benefits . for only twenty months. To minimize this danger, the Society created a uojnouyfond or War Fund. Each adult male member paid an additional 10s per month and each female member paid 5s for the course of the war Although some members complained, arguing that Jednota had enough money. the supreme officers replied that the Society had only enough money to pay 1.000 death benefits President Andrej Pirhalla reassured all members that if they were called to serve in the armed forces, they would not be excluded from membership as long as their friends or relatives continued payments. Because of the war. the Society suspended Paragraphs 25 and 26 of the Bylaws. Paragraph 25 required members to visit sick fellow branch members in the hospital orat home, and Paragraph 26 required all members, under a penalty of S2.00, to attend the funeral of a fellow branch member. Since factories and mines were working overtime it was impossible for all members to fulfill these requirements. The fear of a large number of war deaths thankfully did not materialize. In all. about 7,000 members served in the American and Canadian armed forces during the war. froKj 1917 to 1918. Of this number about 33 were killed in action and another 36 died while in the service. The following. k-dnota members w:r; killed in action or died from wounds Micha) Adzima. Br. 10. Olyphant. Pennsylvania — Killed in France. May 27. 1918. Jozef Bialko. Br. 427. Cleveland. Ohio — Killed in France. August 31. 1918. The movie "Eleni." based on the family story of Nicholas Gage of the New York Times, poignantly describes the horror of the Communist regime In Greece. As long as that movie lives, the love and longing of Gage's mother will never be forgotten. She laid down her own life, to save her sons. Sutherland, Ph.D. JEDNOTA’S HONOR ROLL — WORLD WAR I forty years from now. Today as then, the "trendies" are procommunist. Ed Asner in Hollywood la riding on a train that goes back seventy years, to those who trusted Lenin. Every generation creates its own Lenin. Every generation finds its own disillusionment. Only in future years — when the Sandinistas are no more — . will we find out what f agreements they made with Castro and the USSR: what deceptions they planned and how they executed them; the secrets of their private lives: and their crimes against humanity and against their own people. They may have absolute power now. But absolute power corrupts. One day. those corruptions will be the theme of newspaper revelations, books, and movies. Today, through, led by Da- vid Bonlor (D.. Mich.) in the ” House and Christopher (may his father, the late Senator, forgive him) Dodd (D.. Conn.) tn the Senate, many Democrats In Congress think so well of Daniel Ortega of Nicaragua that they are willing to sell the Democratic Party In order to save him. They want to stop aiding the rebels who are bound gradually to defeat Ortega if supported steadily at moderate levels. And maybe soon, very soon. When dictators fall, like the Shah and Marcos, the fall comes suddenly. Ortega is certainly afraid of . defeat. He has said that the < coming vote In Congress on contra aid is "the most decisive'' moment in the history of the Sandinista Revolution. One may take that to mean that the rebels are no idle threat. Even though the rebels received only 8100 million for one year, they put the Sandinistas (who last year received 8600 million from the Soviets! In a state of alarm. Kill aid to the rebels. Democrats like Benfor and Dodd are saying. Turn them over to the Sandinistas. Hang all 15.000 of them out to dry. Let them — and their families — twist slow ly in the wind. The Important thing is to stop the fighting- Capitulate to the Sandinistas. Let Daniel Ortega rule. Distinguished legislators Bonlor and Dodd do not seem to realize that they are tying their own reputations forevermore to the atrocities yet to be committed In the name of the one-party. Leninist state of Nicaragua. . i If President Reagan loses the * I vote on contra aid. President Ortega wins. Bonior and Dodd can take their pick: Reagan or Ortega President Reagan will be gone after January 20. 1989. But President Ortega may be in power longer than Fidel Castro — and commit as many crimes. Long before January 20. 1989, however, comes the election of November 8. 1988. Suppose that distinguished legisla- ' tors Bonior and Dodd succeed this February In cutting contra aid In Congress. That gives Daniel Ortega. the Leninist Bayardo Arce, and head of the secret police Tomas Borge all of March. April. May. June. July. August. September, and October, to think of something that will humiliate the United States , of America. For eight years this battle has been Ronald Reagan's. If the Democrats kill his program now. they will be held responsible for what happens next. If , they let it continue for the few * months until November. Reagan will be held responsible. But the Democrats no longer think clearly. Every school child in Nicaragua learns to sing the Sandinista national anthem, whose last line hurls a curse at "the Yankee, enemy of humankind." Like the Ayatollah Khomeini, the Sandinistas think of Bonior. Dodd, and all the rest of us as ú "the Great Satan." ’ Bonior and Dodd arc happy to carry water for the Sandinistas. and to wear upon their own backs a sign that reads: "KICK ME." (Christopher Motto) By Father John Catoir Director of The Christophers ŕ i* CONFIRMATION IS ABOUT SURVIVAL The Christophers have Just produced a 22-minutc video on the Sacrament of Confirmation to celebrate that special time in the lives ol our young people when they come of age. We also wanted to help teachers galvanize their students' enthusiasm for the special sacrament they are about to receive. With the help of skilled musicians we've incorporated a contemporary sound in the musical segments, and presented the basic theological concepts in simple, understandable English. As children grow up they are searching for values and attitudes which they will carry with them Into their adult life. The strain they are experiencing growing up is at times enormous and we've tried to offer them some ideas to help tliem along the way Everyone wants to be happy, bul happiness is not something we can grasp directly. Happiness is the by-product of a meaningful life, and preparing for a meaningful life doesn't happen automatically. Many adults are still struggling to find their way. In this video we make the case that a meaningful life is a life of service- Only with God's grace can this truth become acceptable. Just as the winter Ice cap needs the warm spring sun to make it thaw, we human beings need God's grace to subdue our egotism enough to become persons of dignity and worth. The Sacrament of Confirmation conveys the special grace most needed during the maturing process. Coping with peer pressure requires courage. Avoiding the temptation to do drugs calls for wisdom and moral strength. Breaking out of depression, and shrugging off the first thought of suicide demands vigilance and hope. In Confirmation, the grace of fortitude is given to protect those who sincerely receive It. Confirmation is about survival. At baptism someone else made the necessary promises for us. but when we come of age, wc must confirm those promises for ourselves. "Do you renounce Satan? And all his allurements?" After 71 years, the Slovak Catholic Sokol Printery cloaca its doors: With the January 13. 1988 issue, the KATOLICKY SOKOL (Catholic Falcon), official organ of the Slovak Catholic Sokol, was printed for the last time after 70 years in its own Sokol printcry. It will hereafter be printed by an outside press and the name will be changed to SLOVAK CATHOLIC FALCON. The Sokol Printery was opened in January of 1917. Its official organ began as a supplement in 1910 of SLOVAK u AMERIKE, an independent Slovak newspaper, which this year begins its 99th year of publication. On April 15. 1911. the first issue of the KA TOL1CKY SOKOL came off the press at the old International Press, which at that time printed several Slovak publications At the 1915 Convention, the delegates voted to establish their own printery. At one time, the printery published its newspaper and other Sokol publications, but also printed the official publications of other Slovak fraternals and religious communities and other publications as well. It Is a sad day to see another Slovak printery and publishing house fade from the scene after 71 years of being an effective force for the Slovaks in America What is encouraging is the fact that official organ, now called the SLOVAK CATHOLIC FALCON, will still be published and will serve its members, fratemalism, and the Slovak people. What helped to close the printery were rising costs and the government looking for unr^'ated business income by tax-exempt fraternals — Credit; Daniel F. Tanzone, Editor of the SLOVAK CATHOLIC SOKOL In reference to the tax-free status referred to above, the FCSU Board of Directors took on that problem and established Jednota, Inc., a Holding Company with three subsidiaries for profit, thus keeping the Society in Its tax-free status and providing supplementary income That income is very essential because the lower rates on fraternal insurance will not be sufficient for the Society to maintain its present status of providing the many benefits to the members and the Slovak people The three subsidiaries of the Holding Company are: Jednota Properties. Inc., Jednota General. Inc., and Jednota Communications, Inc. As an example, the Jednota Press is under the Jednota Communications. Inc., whose board members are: Thomas J. Guidish. F.I.C.. Vice President of the FCSU. Chairman; Richard Salamon. Treasurer; Andrew Beno. Secretary: Cecilia Sarocky. and Robert Krohmaly. The FCSU Board of Directors deserves our commendation for taking these steps into a new era for our Society. Our Slovak Heritage: One of the more famous Slovak immigrants at the turn of the century was the Reverend Joseph Murgas. He made the first radio broadcast In the world in 1904. from Wilkes-Barre. Pennsylvania, to Scranton. Pennsylvania, before those of Marconi. The first words broadcast by Father Murgas. known as the Radio Priest, were those of a sentimental poem in a form of greeting which began. Fly. fly waves to the country' of my birth." There were no radio stations in Slovakia at this time, however, but the genius of Father Murgas is a tribute to the Slovak ethnic identity of the famous inventor, in whom even President Theodore Roosevelt expressed his great interest. IN MEMORIAM THE REV, JOHN JOSEPH SPITKOVSKY (1928 — 1987) On the Jednota Family's first great Picnic Day. We met you at Middletown on a gala holiday. The crowds were Jubilant in verily a spiritual way; Buses, cars and passengers arrived without delay. The Fraternal Family spirit made it a perfect day: Top officials, delegates and friends came to pray. The Chaplain celebrated Holy Mass for every devote. Twas a duty performed as a noted, grand celebre. The inspiring Homily and Communicants were egalite. The memories most impressive for time and eternity. Alas, time takes its own toll, day after day: We're grieved to learn. "Death takes no Holiday!" The young Supreme Chaplain, later pastor, died suddenly. Twas a fabulous loss to Jednota's Fraternity. The "Priestly Priest" — beloved by all — lives eternally. A Good Shepherd, so gifted, now lives for history. His loving smile; wisdom and modesty; became his legacy. "The Book of Life" records his beloved labors totally. He left his mark of success in time's sands with simplicity: While he rejoices forever with a most glorious victory. This zealous, wise and Joyful priest, ere soon forgotten! Let us pray to him ever before God's heavenly Fountain. A solemnly. stately and stalworth Slovak son of dignity: Grant we beseech him. for us: "Unity tn Diversity." May he. with the Eternal High Priest, banish the enemy: Uniting, his fellowmen. suffering and sorrowing, in pity- Granting peace and concord to Slovakia's Church in adversity: Then, we'll laud his name ever for "God and Country. Halil Noble son of Slovakian ancestry. In all humility! We salute you for ever as "The Smiling Priest' ‘ in Eternity! "Sing to the Lord a new song. for he has done marvelous deeds. " R I P. (Ps. 98:1) Fr. John Joseph Tomatonls Catholic Press Month 1988 VATICAN CITY (NC) — Here is the text of a statement by Archbishop John P. Foley, president of the Pontifical Commission for Social Communications, on Catholic Press Month 1988. "Collaborators in truth ..." That's what Pope John Paul II called Catholic publications and journalists in the Catholic press during his recent visit to the United States. The Catholic press communicates the truth of the Gospel and the truth about a world which needs the saving word of their Gospel. Catholics need a dependable source of authentic information about Christ's church, their church, and about the moral questions which fill their lives every day. They need to know how they can live close to God in their families and in their work: they need to know about the needs of others — the hungry, the oppressed, the afflicted — so that they can help in the name of Christ and so that they don't become selfish themselves. "Collaborators in truth in the service of justice, fairness and love ... " That's the full description Pope John Paul used for Catholic communicators. All Catholic journalists Work with the Holy Father to communicate the fullness of truth and lo stimulate fidelity to a life of integrity and of charity. All Catholics need this continuing formation and stimulation if they are to be mature in faith and in fervor. The Catholic press remains the most effective instrument we have for ongoing formation in a timeless faith and for continuing stimulation to ever-needed charity. -IF WE DIDN’T HAVE A SLOVAK CATHOLIC PRESS. The measure would overturn a 1984 Supreme Court ruling that only programs receiving federal funds in such institutions as universities, not the entire institution, can be penalized for violations of civil rights laws. Pro-lifers worry that the bill also would reinforce a federal government administrative interpretation that Title IX. part of a civil rights education law. demands provision of abortion services. FEBRUARY 3, 1988 WORLD COMMUNICATIONS DAY (Continued from Page 1) j? ' recognized a brother in the man who was wounded, in contrast to the priest and the Levite (cf. Lk 10:29-27). In like manner, the apostle Paul invites us not to despise the gifts of others, but to rejoice in the Spirit's workings in each of our brothers (1 Cor 12:14-30) Fraternity and solidarity are basic and urgent things: They ought to be. in our times, distinguishing marks of peoples and cultures. The joyous unveiling of their happy, friendly, helpful relationships with one another: Ought this not to be the most beautiful "festival" offered by mass communications. their most successful "show." in the best sense of these terms? In this period ol spectacular development in mass communications, the bonds which they weave between peoples and cultures represent the most precious and valuable support that they offer to humanity. I know, at the same time, that you communicators are aware of perverse effects as well, which threaten distortion and disfigurement to these relations between peoples and cultures. Exaltation of the sell, contempt for and rejection of those who are different can aggravate tensions and divisions. From such attitudes spring violence, mistaken directions, destruction ol true communication; for they render impossible all brotherly relationship. 3. InArdy.thatthe very existence of brotherhood and human solidarity may be ma^p^&ible. and still more so that their Christian dimension may be mofeintensely developed, the elementary values which underlie them have to be given recognition Permit me here to recall certain of these: respect for others, a willingness to dialogue, justice, healthy ethics in personal and community living, freedom, equality, peace in unity, promotion ol the dignity ol the human person, ths capacity to share and to divide with others. Brotherhood and solidarity rise above all clannish and corporation spirit, all nationalism, all racism, every abuse of power, every individual fanaticism, be it cultural or religious. II is lor the agents of mass communication to use the techniques and media al their disposal always with a conscientious regard to these primary values. Here I suggest only some of the possible applications: — Let the information agencies and the whole ensemble of the press demonstrate their respect for others by giving complete and balanced information. — The radiophonic diffusion of the word will better attain its purpose in the measure in which the possibility of reciprocal exchange is offered to all. — Media which are the mouthpiece of particular groups contribute to the operation of justice in giving a voice also to the voiceless. — Television programs touch on almost all aspects of life, and the networks have countless possible applications and interconnections: Considering their great influence, there must rest on those responsible for them an ethical imperative, obliging them to offer to persons and communities images which iavor the mutual penetration of cultures, without intolerance and without violence, in the service of unity. — The possibility of personal communications by telephone, their extension by teletext, their wider and wider spread by satellite, all this suggests that we should have a care lor the equality among persons and facilitate access to these media to a greater number, so as to permit really meaningful exchanges. — Information increasingly concerns economic or cultural activities; data banks bring together a quantity of diverse information which was unimaginable in earlier times: It is known that this could be used in such a way as Io bring al! sorts of pressure Io bear on private or community life; and the wise management of these media thus becomes a veritable precondition of peace. — To create (he "shows" which sustain the various audiovisual media implies an obligation to respect the consciences of the countless "spectators" in the audiences. — Advertising publicity awakens or increases desires and also creates needs: Those who commission it or bring it to realization have to keep in mind the disadvantaged for whom the benefits advertised are out of reach Whatever the exact mode by which they intervene, it is necessary that the communicators observe a code of honor, that they have a care for the sharing out of the truth among and about humankind, and that they shaft contribute Io a new world order of information and of communication 4. In the midst of the ever more concentrated and active network of social communications spanning the world, the church binds itself simply to keep on ceaselessly recalling, as "an expert in humanity," lhe values which constitute the grandeur of the human being. But she is at the same time convinced that these cannot be assimilated and put into action in the concrete, if the spiritual lifeol the human being is forgotten. For Christians, the revelation of God in Christ is an illumination on man and woman, on humanity itself. Faith in the message of salvation constitutes the most deeply felt motivation for serving the human race. The gifts ol the Spirit engage us in the service of others in a brotherly solidarity. The question may be asked ol us: Are we not perhaps too trusting in our openness to this kind of thinking? Do the tendencies which are apparent in the field of mass communication really permit us to entertain such hopes’ To hearts which are troubled by the risks ol the new technologies of communication. I would reply: "Fear noil" Far from ignoring the reality in which we live, we read it more profoundly. We distinguish, in the light of faith, the authentic signs of the times. The church, full of care for man and woman, is aware of the deep aspiration of the human race for fraternity and solidarity — an aspiration often disowned or disfigured, but indestructible because it is sculpted in the human heart by the same God who has created m it the need for communication and the capacity to develop it on a planetary scale. 5. On the threshold of the third millennium, the church reminds men and women that fraternity and solidarity cannot be regarded merely as con dilions for survival; they are essentials of their vocation, a vocation which the exercise of social communication permits them to realize freely. Permit me then to say to you all, especially in this Marian year "Do not be afraid! " Was not Mary herself frightened at the announcement which contained the sign of salvation offered to the whole of humanity? "Blessed is she who believed." as Elizabeth testified (Lk 1:45). It is thanks to her faith that the Virgin Mary accepts God's plan, enters into the mystery of trinitarian communion, and, becoming the mother of Christ, inaugurates in history a new fraternity Blessed are those who believe, those whom the faith liberates from fear and opens to hope and leads to the construction of a world where, in fraternity and solidarity, there is place for the communication of joy! Animated by this profound joy for the gifts of communication received for the building-up of all. in this solid fraternity, I invoke on each one of you the blessing of the Most High. From the Vatican. Jan. 24. 1988. the feast of St. Francis de Sales. Catholic Press Month (Continued from Page 1) . Whether we communicate orally, electronically or. as I am doing now, by little black marks on paper, communication enables us to allow minds to meet and hearts to speak to hearts. Some are more skilled than others in the art of communication. The Catholic communicator must possess the same skills which any journalist or writer must have. But it is the substance of the message which sets the Catholic press apart from the secular press The Catholic press has the special task ol announcing to the world good tidings of great joy. Even when, as service of truth occasionally demands, the news may be depressing, it is always proclaimed against the backdrop of the good news of our salvation, Even when the news is of a fallen nature, the Catholic writer and reader are keenly aware of redemption. The God whom we serve is a God not only of truth, but also of justice, fairness, and love. Therefore if we serve justice, fairness, and love we serve God. Catholic Press Month affords journalists and writers an opportunity to reflect on their art and their mission. Readers, especially those in the United States, might use this month to thank God that the CaihcĚc press is free to set its own agenda and to write as it chooses. Truth makes us free and we are grateful for this freedom. Our brothers and sisters in lands fess free know that it is the nature of tyrants to oppose freedom. One of the first freedoms which comes under attack is the freedom of the press and other communication channels. It would be well this month to pray for our brothers and sisters who live in a dark world without the light of a free press. The days of February are short and dark and often cold. But we rejoice that these days are brightened and warmed by the words of the Catholic press. What a blessing! How beautiful are the feet of those who bring good news of peace. (Bsrop Bosco s tr« Honorary Pres.dor.1 cf trie Carole Press Assoc aeon ri the Unred Stales and Canada and the Chairman cf the Comm.-nee cn Corr mur catons for the United Stales Galnofic Conference.) Rumor of Papal Trip (Continued from Page 1) Bishop Sapelak visited in 1986 and afterward expressed hope for the restoration of the Ukrainian church’s legality. He also said the church is beginning to come above ground with the knowledge ol local authorities. Last year several Ukrainian bishops and priests released a joint letter saying they would begin exercising their ministry in the open. The implication of all these activities is that some thaw in Vatican- Soviet relations is taking place But does this mean a papal visit? Right now. the answer is a firm "no." Speculation has centered on next June's celebrations in Moscow organized by the Russian Orthodox Church to commemorate the 1,000th anniversary of the establishment of Christianity in what is now the Soviet Union. This has been fueled by occasional reports from Moscow that Orthodox officials may have the OK to invite the pope. The theory behind these reports is that photos of a smiling pope at a religious ceremony in Moscow would be a propaganda coup for the Soviet Union, a weapon to parry complaints of religious restrictions. The latest lest of this theory occurred at an impromptu Jan. 17 papal press conference with foreign journalists in Rome The pope was asked if he planned to attend the millennium celebrations. Asinthepasthe responded that he has yet to be invited, and then tad out his terms lor the type of invitation he would accept. These include permission to make pastoral visits to Catholics in various Soviet republics. The pope noted that a main obstacle to this is the illegal status ol the Ukrainian Catholic Church and restrictions on Catholicism in other parts of the Soviet Union. The pope previously had stated these conditions to inquiring reporters. As before, the conditions did not go down well with Soviet officials. Two days later a Soviet foreign ministry spokesman reiterated that the government has no plans to invite the pope. This was followed by government press articles justifying the illegal status of the Ukrainian church and criticizing it as a "Nazi- fascist" organization. Yet the fad remains that something is happening in Vatican-Soviet relations Maybe the clue is in the papa! conditions for the visit Church emissaries, rather than negotiating a papal tnp, may weil be testing out the political will of the new Soviet leadership to substantially loosen religious restrictions as part of a general overhaul of society. Under this theory, until they do. Soviet authorities will not get their photos taken smiling with the pope in Moscow. Principal Aíjn PARIS (NC) — The French Catholic Church has joined Protestant and Orthodox churches in forming the country's first Council ol Christian Churches. In a statement, the 18-member council said its principal aim is to give the churches a united voice on major social problems. It said it will take common In-' Natives in social issues, charitable service and Christian wriness. The statement specifically mentioned unemployment. raasm and acquired immune deficiency syndrome as problems the council would address. Coural rules require a unanimous vote ol the members before a stand is taken on important issues. FEAST OF SS. CYRIL AND METHODIUS (Continued from Page 1) The First Reading on this feast mentions God's messengers, whom the Lord sends to unbelieving and sinful mankind. InHis Providence God :'r commissioned SS. Cyril and Methodius to christianize the Slavic nations, Q j These brother-apostles may be accounted our greatest benefactors ' because they were instrumental in proclaiming the faith to our forefathers. 1. The Lord sends His messengers. In the history of salvation it is commonly noted that God does not”' ’ employ only His angels to accomplish His designs; He singles out men and women as well, among them the prophets, to announce His messages to the people of God (II. Par. 36.15; Job 33.32). Among such messengers ol God were, for example: Abraham, Moses. Jeremiah. Elijah. Eliseus, . Judith and others. The message which these heralds conveyed to the world • was ordinarily the disclosure of God's will in particular cases. At times God admonished the people to abandon sinful ways. Sometimes He laid down the conditions under which He was prepared to deliver them from captivity or from the hands of persecutors In the First Reading of this Mass the prophet Isaiah somehow synthesizes the messages of all ol God's heralds in Salvation History up to | and including St. John the Baptist hiriiself. Isaiah refers to their message as "good news" because substantially he announces the redemption of a sinful generation from the slavery of sin. The precise words of Isaiah are: "How beautiful are the feet of him who brings glad tidings, announcing peace, bearing good news, announcing salvation, and saying to Zion. 'Your God is King.' " (Is. 52. 7) The Gospel of this feast highlights God’s Messenger of all messengers, the Son of God. Jesus Chnsl. who actualized the predicted salvation for the whole worfd. Jesus also included the future in His blessed plan, the time when He would leave the earth and return to His Father in heaven. In order to benefit all peoples of all times and actualize salvation lor . every generation He established His church, structuring it on His first missionaries, His twelve apostles. They indeed proclaimed the Gospel of Christ. Sf. Luke writes lhal the aposlles "departed and walked from village to .. village, preaching and healing" (Lk. 9,6). When Christ saw that the "harvest # is great but the laborers are few" (Lk.’1O, 2). he appointed seventy-two disciples to assist [he apostles and to proclaim the Kingdom’of God with them In the incident related in today's Gospel, Christ cautions them that Iheir work will be far from easy. "Behold I am sending you as lambs among wolves " In the preceding paragraph Christ had invited them to devote themselves completely to apostolic work, to renounce Iheir families and relatives, to face injustice, because "He who puts his hand to the plow and looks back is not worthy of the Kingdom of God" (Lk. 9. 62). Following our Lord’s ascension into heaven, the Catholic Church from its very inception fulfilled Christ’s command (Mt. 28,18 20) and sent God's messengers, her missionaries, to all nations to proclaim the good news of Christ's redemption to them. 2. God sent SS. Cyril and Methodius to our forefathers. History relates that SS. Cyril and Methodius in their native Greece lived as religious, serving God by Iheir holy and exemplary lives. When the Emperor expressed his wish that they undertake the conversion of the people of Great Moravia and announce the Gospel message to them, their hearts were stirred with apostolic love and zeal. They recalled Christ's words in the Gospel: "The harvest is great but the laborers are few." They ac- $ cepted their mission with God-inspired enthusiasm. In their mission of christianizing our forefathers, SS. Cynl and Methodius were greatly aided by their translation into Slovak of lhe Latin liturgy and the holy gospel. Without difficulty, the people were able to grasp lhe fundamental truths of Christianity. The success of SS. Cynl and Methodius with the Slovak people led the Vatican Council II to acknowledge them as patrons of a vernacular liturgy, proclaiming their merits to the whole world. The apostolic spirit of SS. Cyril and Methodius was not without its effect on the Slovak immigrants who. driven from iheir homeland by the harassments of life, found their way al last to a free America. Noteworthy among these hardy pioneers was Father Matthew Jankola, founder of Hie1’? Congregation of the Sisters of SS. Cyril and Methodius. To the care of these " devoted religious he confided the children of Slovak immigrants as well as persons of advanced age. The Slovak nation took note of this historic accomplishment in 1972 when it commemorated the centenary of the birth of Father Jankola. For all these benefits we praise and magnify SS. Cyrif and Methodius in the words ol the Second Reading of their feast: "Speak we now in honor of. famous men that were our fathers, long ago. What high achievements the Lord has made known in them . . issuing to the people the commands ’ their times needed, uttering, through their foresight, a sacred charge to the nations ... this line of theirs will endure for all lime; iheir stock, their,, name, will never be allowed to die oul." (Sir. 44. 1-15) The heritage of SS. Cyril and Methodius is a bond that unites all Slavic -. peoples today. By their mission God invited us to live as His children, filled with holy hope and worthy of eternal merit. The fruit of holy faith, which. we inherited from our forefathers, must be manifest in our lives. We must be strong, patient and sacrificing in our faith, in imitation of SS. Cyril and Methodius who spent themselves for us. Only in this way shall we pass . on the faith to those who will come after us. “ * The deep significance of this feast obliges us to place a high value?- on our sacred gift ol faith and not to yield to modern laxity and confusion./ Alas, some Christians in cur day seem to be ol the opinion that they have '* satisfied their debt to God for the gift of faith by singing pious hymns in church once a week or even perhaps by receiving Holy Communion, but Chnsl Himself they keep locked up behind the golden door of the tabernacle. If. however, we wish in some way to manifest our gratitude Io God for the precious gift of laith. we must allow our faith to permeate our entire being. We must submit our very judgment to God so that within us il will be God who thinks, it will be God who acts so as to lead us to a sincere love of Himself, of our heavenly Father and our neighbor. Meanwhile we admit that without God we can accomplish nothing for salvation (John 15. 4- 5). Living our faith vigorously we shall thus repay our apostles SS. Cyril and Methodius for their life’s sacrifices which they endured for our salvation. In our day also the words of Christ apply: "The harvest is great, but the laborers are few." The world is bleeding today from wounds, perhaps self-inflicted, and thirsting for Christ's saving grace. As God’s blessed messengers, SS. Cyril and Methodius brought us a heavenly anchor, our holy faith; out of gratitude let us profess our faith lovingly and courageously, thus praising God and bringing hope today to many who have no hope. A Valentine A valentine expresses pleasant memories tn a way, finds words the heart exactly wants loudly to say. That one wants to share the melodies that you sing, be of service and love to everyone and everything. That God's love made a sacrifice for souls like you. to lighten crosses and prove that His love Is true. it sends our thoughts heavenward, so happy and sweet, makes life such a reality and on the ground our feet. That the beauty of creation is neither yours or mine, only a chip of eternal love Is found In a valentine. OK 73069 The following day. the pope visited Rome's St. Gabriel Parish and continued stressing Ihe importance of marriage. ■ 'The witness of grace and joy of the sacrament of marriage is the first and strongest answer that Ihe Christian family can give to those who have chosen forms of union outside the sacrament," the pope said in his Mass homily. Prior to the Mass, the pope spoke to teachers and students of the parish school and said church schools should be open to all economic classes. The aim is "to offer, even to those with little economic means, Ihe guarantee to obtain the level of culture possible to them, according to their gifts and' aspirations," the pope said During the visit, parishioners gave the pope a par of skis. “I have more skis than occasions to go skiing." said the pope, who several times during his pontificate has taken days off to ski in the Italian mountains. Earlier in the day, the pope blessed several thousand members of Rome's Catholic Action youth movement, participants in a month- long study and reflection program on peace issues. The youths were in St. Peter's Square lo hear the pope's midday Angelus talk. Youths can work for peace by "educating yourselves to love of truth, of honesty, ol justice, of respect for neighbor, and of generosity." the pope said from his apart ment window overlooking St. Peter's Square. FEBRUARY 3. 1988 Spider Webs in Hostetter, Pennsylvania A contributor to this column in the past. August E. Wasco from Breckville. Ohio, is once again furnishing the readers ol ihe JEDNOTA with a glimpse into the life of a Slovak community years ago In his words "II is good to hear that Hostetler was mentioned in a previous issue of the JEDNOTA No one can imagine what it was like in a small mining town m Pennsylvania, unless they had lived there; especially if it was a quiet law and order town such as Hostetter. While there may have been litlie excitement. I remembgtma incident that proved to be out ol the ordinary. Back tíieřrTWin used the straight razor lor shaving. And when they accident- ady cuHhElfl^lves. they may have used a piece ol talst paper or an aseptic pencil Io slop the bleeding One day a small girl, who was trom a large family (large families were quite common in our town), pounded on our kitchen door and yelled, 'Mrs. Wasco! Mrs Wasco1’ When my’mother opened the door, the excited youngster asked my mother if she could give her some spider webs. To me it was a cunous request soil seemed al the lime. Nevertheless, my mother knew what it was about and she searched every comer of Ihe house with a strek. As soon as my mom gave the little girl a handfull of the webs, she hurnedfy ran home My mother turned to me and said ’Come on. we had better go to see what has happened and maybe we can help" When we approached the house a group of people had gathered by the gate ol ihe picket fence. Not being able to understand what was being said, for they were conversing in Sovak, 1 pulled on my molher s apron and asked what was gang on. She then told me that the man who lived in that house had cut himself while using a straight razor. The spider webs were being used to stop the bleeding and would also help in the healing process Later on she told me that some of the people believed that ihe cut was infhcted by someone who wanted to do him in The gossip continued for several days, afferwhich it no longer held their interest " Before people even realized that spider webs contained penicillin, they had used the webs quite effectively to heal wounds. There were cases reported where a severed finger was rejoined and wrapped in spider webs and then bandaged, wth healing taking place as a result. Hostetter, Pennsylvania Religious Vocation Fund Established BUFFALO. N.Y (NC) — The Palisano Religious Vocation Fund has been established to loan money lor college to candidates to the priesthood and religious life in Ihe Buffalo Diocese The fund was established by trustees of the Vincent and Hamel PaLsano Foundation to encourage religious vocations by providing the financial means lo obtain a bachelor s degree a requirement for acceptance into the major seminary and lor entrance mlo many religious congregations The fund was established at Canisius College, a Jesuit college in Buffalo, but may be extended to other area Cathdic colleges in the future New Rates — JEDNOTA Founded May PUBLISHED EVERY WEDNESDAY Joseph C. Box 150, Middletown, PA 17057 (717) 944-0461 A First for Cleveland Slovaks Slovak Display at the Cleveland Museum of Art By Veronica Ratica Hipp This past fall bore witness to a marvelous and long-overdue ' fust for Slovaks and other Eastern European ethnic groups in the Cleveland area; the Cleveland Museum of Art. rn cooperation wth the Roberson Museum of Binghamton. New York, sponsored a four-week long display of embroideries and textiles from Eastern Europe. The exhibit, called "Goddesses and Their Offspring — Nineteenth and Twentieth Century Eastern European Embroideries." focused on ethnic vana lions ol the goddess motif, an ancient Indo-European embroidery motif best preserved among Slavs. This representation ol ‘earth mother' and 'fertility” has existed since pre-Christian times and is still evident in ethnic dress, pottery, Easter egg design, and other talk art expressions from Eastern Europe. Along with the Slovak display, there was presented a display from the following Slavic/lndo-European groups: Croatian. Hungarian. Polish, Romanian. Russian, Ruthenian.. Slovenian, and Ukrainian. The Slovak Heritage and Folklore Society, an organization which was formed just last year and which publishes the newsletter Slovakia. had the honor of donating artifacts tor the Slovak display and providing a key speaker on one of two activity weekends at the Beck Center in Lakewood, where the exhibit was shown Ms Helene Cincebox, editor of Slovakia and founder A Slovak cardboard "mannequin" exhibiting ceremonial clothing such as wristlets, a mcnik, and chest covering, all bearing the goddess motif, and used here as a symbol for "protection" of the newborn. Entrance view of the exhibit features two Slovak ladies at the turn of the century, a period of transition due to Immigration and Industrialization. This transition with textiles is evidenced In their “modem" late 19th century dress but traditional cepec head covering. of the society, presented a slide presentation entitled "Folk Art Treasures of Slovakia " Veronica Ratica Hipp, secretary of the society, worked with the museum's Extensions Division Curator, Andrew Chakalis. to assemble a display of the following items from the 19th and 20th centuries from Slovakia: a complete kroj from Trenčianske Tepla that featured an apron motif of a goddess on horseback, trom Ihe 17th century in style, and the rarest goddess motif in the entire exhibit, a man's elaborate kožuch with the tree of life goddess motif; several embroidered and batik aprons with stylized goddess motif; a wedding shawl and several ceremonial ručníky with various fertility symbols; a man's wedding shirt trom Hepla with stylized fertility and goddess symbols; and an elaborate viencok and beaded bridal cap. both symbols ol the ancient goddess. Because of the enthusiasm with which this exhibit was received, and because of the excellent cooperation trial the museum received from various groups wanting to display their artifacts, Mr. Chakalis stated that this was a venture initially "approached with caution by the museum but obviously long overdue and in need of repetition." Mr Chakalis also stated that this proved, first and foremost, that a definite and great need exists for the establishment of a multi-ethnic museum in Cleveland.' The wall to the entrance of the exhibit displays the groups and countries/ter- ritories represented. Tribute to Msgr. J. Rekem On His 70th Birthday By Dr. Jozef M. Kirschbsum On December 13th of last year Msgr Jan Rekem. STD. a dedicated defender of Slovak national traditions and a prolific writer on cultural and literary history, celebrated his 70th birthday. Born m Trenčianska Tepla. Slovakia. Msgr. Rekem completed high school and theology m Nitra and was ordained a priest in 1941 by Archbishop Karol Kmetko He successfully fulfilled his priestly SPAS (KARLOVY-VARY • PIEŠŤANY) ZA Wls Nun: $1090 W S1ĚSÍ R/T AIR • HOTELS • MEALS • SPAS PRICE INCLUDES; (ppid) • IVr AWARE Ya CSA Airlines • SPA TieatmentsiDi Supervised • HOTEL .3 mas tey • E<®e«e ■ Trusi • hafW Toms or individual travel Bes! low cost nwwatile. vacations Via CSA Airtines Free brochures. At ine same time he was involved in cultural and social activities as co- edtor o: newspapers and almanacs and contributor to cultural period ■ cals. For these activities he was arrested m 1945 and spent 0 months in prison Before another imminent arrest m 1947. he escaped to Aus tna where he became active among Slovak refugees. In 1949 he emi grated to Canada He became active in Canada as he had been in Slovakia, not only as a priest by organizing a new Slovak parish in Winnipeg and building a church, but also as a contributor to Slovak periodicals and newspapers published in the various countries of the free world tn 1962 he added to the list of his 13 works published m Slovakia by writing several well-documented studies in English These works, released either as reprints or in book form, have been praised by Canadian Slavists. Three ol them. The Origin and Development of the Slovak Language (Middletown. Pa , 1962). Slovak Literature and national Consciousness Before Anton Bernolak (Middletown. Pa, 1965) and Selected Chapters From Slovak Cultural History (Rome, 1969). are standard works in the field of Slavic studies Between 1962 and 1966 he also published monographs on three Slovak painters — Stefan Straka, EAKLY FOR EASTER KOLACHKI c o o k bo o k ' palletgmn- pgrcMail pcg«a wllh ruby red ilialtrallúnd-7^ by 10 j boWiy r” NOTJUST ANOTHERCOOKBOOK - aperfed krepakctiľl ol rtvipn ipanning o,s< jOyan — beauillully ,llu>iralcd — tsiy la read — duiMm; deluded imlruaions — a liitle hiUoey PcltM for M* «oks RKitKl ror KOLACHKI made w>lh Thousands Sold.'! WEDNESDAY. FEBRUARY 3, 1988 ~ * Plans Set for International Jednota Handicap Bowling Tournament in 1988 By George Yakubisln. FCSU Director of Fraternal Activities Bowling Results Plans have been set lor the 34th Annual Men s and the 25th Annual Women's International Jednota Handicap Ten Pin Bowling Tournament scheduled for the Brunswick 51 Lanes in Belle Vernon, Pennsylvania hosted by Branch 670 K.J. in Donora. Pennsylvania Entry blanks for these events have been mailed to last year 's team captains and to the Jednota Athletic Promotional Committee members for distribution in their local areas, Anyone who has not yet received the entry forms please send a card to the tournament secretary. Paul Ritz, 44 Palmer Ave.. Campbell, Ohio 44405 and you will receive the forms. Please do not delay so that you may receive the times and weekends when your team wishes to participate. All events will be at the Brunswick 51 Lanes. Route 51. South Belle Vernon, Pennsylvania The team events will be scheduled for Friday. Apnl 8 and 15.1988 at 6 p.m. and 9 p.m.. and err Saturday. April 9 and 16, 1988 at 3:15 p.m. The double and single events are scheduled for Saturday. April 9 and 16.1988 at 8:30 a m. and 11:45 a.m. and on Saturday. Aoril 10 apd 17. 1988 at 9:45 a.m.. 12:30 p m. and 3:15 p.m We asiľ^Sefjrfôam captain or District captain to choose the weekends and dates and times thgT-teifri wishes to bowl. We want to formulate a schedule to suit all participants. Should all the dates and times that are requested be filled then the teams will receive the shifts available coinciding with the chosen times. We ask that all teams and districts be flexible. We have only so many lanes to work wilh. The entry fee lor each event is $9.00 The total cost for single, double and team events is $27.00, To be eligible for all events one must submit an additional $2.00 which would make the total entry fee for each participant $29.00 We must receive.the entire entry lee with the submission of Ihe entry forms All who have advance reservations must submit entry forms and entry fees no later than March 1.1988. If the forms and fees are not submitted by that date, the requested times and shifts will be given to other participants. All learns are reminded that they must supply Ihe necessary bowlers. Do not come to the tournament looking for "fill in bowlers." This has created much confusion in the past. We have had problems filling in short teams and double partners. The necessary fill-ins should be brought from one's local area The rules and regulations lor the tournament are pnnted on the entry forms- Please read them carefully since they are the rules sanctioned by the ABC and the WIBS which the bowlers will be playing by. We find that many bowiers and bowferettes fail to read the rules and upon completion of their game discover that they have violated a certain rule and have been eliminated from the win sheet. One must be careful with the ten pins over last year's average. It should show that one has ten pins higher on January 1,1988. One must use the higher average. Accommodations All who have received entry forms have received forms lor motel reservations plus a map showing the location ol the motels, the lanes and the church where Masses will be celebrated. In case of learns of two or more bowlers, the averages shall be combined to determine whether rhe corral total is higher or lower than the submitted to lai. Corrections in averages can be made up to the completion of the first game of a series, or within 48 hours after completion of a series if the tournament manager prior to the completion of the first game of the series has given written consent to the bowler authorizing such extension of time to correct his average. 363-b Any entrant who has qualified for a prize of $300 or more in any event of a tournament tn the 12-month period prior to entry, must report actual score, position and amount won to tournament management at time ol entry for possible terating Pittsburgh District FCSU Men's Division January 8. 1988 W L Sokols 37 23 Savolskis 36 24 Four+Three 36 24 Cuzzin's Cate 33 27 Aey Radio & TV 32ft 27ft A.U. 628 32 27ft Duq. High three games individual, W Drapcho. 600, čMásVWó In Akron, Ohio Slovak-Owned Fairlawn Lanes Will Host 46th SBC Tournament Fairlawn Lanes in the Fairlawn area ol Akron is getting geared up (or the 46th Annual Slovak Bowling Congress of America tournament to be staged weekends, April 9 thru May 1. 1988 The Slovak-National tenpin bowling meet, sanctioned by American Bowling Congress, is expected to play host to some 1.000 men bowlers from 175 alias of Ohio. Illinois, Pennsylvania, Indiana. Michigan. West Virginia, New Mexico, Florida, and the Dominion of Canada who will be competing lor prize money in excess of $13.000. Entries for the Slovak-National tourney will dose on March 5 with Secretary. S.J. Hletko, 6645 West 87lh Place, Oak Lawn. Ill, 60453. Telephone 312-599-8152. Faidawn Lanes houses 30 modern, wen-conditioned lanes, offers excellent tourney facilities and features friendly service and hospitality. This will be Akron's very first opportunity to host the nation's Slovak keglers. The tournament establishment is Slovak-owned and operated by Mrs Louise Horvath and her 3 sons Mrs. Horvath is a native of Cleveland and resides in nearby Independence. She considers it a high honor to host the SBC event. Mrs. Horvath comes from a pioneer Slovak family. Her late father. Joseph Miklovic. was a spirited Slovak activist and Iraternalist. He was truly a Slovak patriarch in the Cleveland area lor many years. He was widely recognized as a Slovak organizer. Born in 1886 in Polodin, Slovakia, he came to America at age 18 in 1905, Almost immediately he became involved in promoting Slovak events, He was always self- employed and was a capable and successful businessman, and was generally interested in encouraging Slovak youth in ethnic activity. His daughter. The Christo- Open Season This is not a nightmarish dream — It came from the US Court Supreme. You have the right to kill. Abort your baby, kill at will. Your unborn child will never know — Cause you didn’t let.him grow. Kill, abort, have no fear. But don't go over your quota of deer. Kill, abort, at a moment's wish. But don’t catch too many fish. Save lhe cute little baby seal. Abort your baby, "He’s not for real." Save the American buffalo, Another child aborted — Here we go. Send us money — save the ducks so wild. Don’t ‘birth’ your baby — abort your child. Save the vanishing crocodile — Throw the aborted on the pile Save the leopard, yes, we can — Abort your child, there is no ban. Wear alligator shoes? — don't you dare! Ils' life, not your baby’s, we will spare. Favor the flamingo, so they won't be rare, Abort your child, have no care. Yes, go on, you're allowed to abort. So says the ruling from the Supreme Court. Protect the WILD LIFE or pay a fee. Kill your BABY and go free.’! The Supreme Court’s ruling — an added strife — Ils OPEN SEASON on your baby’s life. Bertovich Grants Available for Support of Traditional and Ethnic Arts HARRISBURG - In cooperation with the Pennsylvan'a Heritage Affairs Commission. the Pennsylvania Counol on the Arts announces the availability of grant support for tracltonal and ethnic arts Support is available lor the costs of traditional and ethnic music or dance presentation, and projects which pass on traditional muse or dance performance techniques and repertoire to qualified students, tn the area of crafts, support is available for a broad range of programs which involve lhe creation, teaching, exhibition, and documentation of traditional crafts Program guidelines are now available lor "Apprenticeships in Traditional Arts,'' a program which enables recognized "master" performers and crafts- workers to train qualified "apprentices" tor a period of up to one year Traditional arts, such as instrumental muse, song, dance, storytelling, and crafts, have evolved through time within the many groups that make up Pennsylvania's population - groups that share the same ethnic heritage, language. Occupation, religion, or geographic region These arts often serve as symbols of a group's identity or heritage, and communities may take great pride in ther continuity. Traditional arts are generally learned informally, through observation and practice. Arts such as traditional music and dance embody a communal aesthetic with identifiable forms and styles Individual artists may be recognized for iheir exChurch’s Top Marriage Tribunal . VATICAN CITY — Pope John Paul H told the church 's top marnage tribunal to be wary ol overemphasizing "slight" psycholog cal problems when it considers expert assessments of the mental state of couples seeking annulments Contrary to what psychiatric experts define as normal, Christianity understands that a "slight or moderate psychopathology." or mental disorder, does nol necessarily hinder a person's ability to make responsible and free decisions, the pope said in a lengthy talk to the Roman Rota cellence. their extensive reperioireand particular styles of performance. The mastery of these arts envolves far more than techncal proficiency, for this artistry embodies a group's values and aesthetics. Grants are available on a matching basis; however, in the apprenticeship category, organizations may apply for up to $4.000 in non matching funds for fees for master traditional artists, necessary supplies for instructon. travel costs for the master and travel costs for the apprentices, li these costs exceed $4.000. additional funds may be requested on a matching bass Matching funds are also available to support documentation and public programming. in the apprenticeship category, priority will be given to projects (hat are enhanced with public educational programs such as performances, lectures, seminars, or workshops reaching a broad audience and providing a forum for furthering the appreciation of tradi- tional arts within its context as a community-based an form. Organizations that wish to submit an application should contact the Pennsylvania Council on lhe Arts. Room 216 Finance Building. Harrisburg. PA 17120, (717) 787-6883 or the Pennsylvania Heritage Affairs Commission. Slate FolWife Program. 309 Forum Building. Hamsburg. PA 17120.
